<div id="review_3295434281" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3295434281" />
    <a title="Beth Bonini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3106785-beth-bonini"><img alt="Beth Bonini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444482564p2/3106785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3295434281?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth Bonini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth Bonini" href="/user/show/3106785-beth-bonini">Beth Bonini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=fictitious-biography">fictitious-biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=orphans">orphans</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=death-and-old-age">death-and-old-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=20th-century-canadian">20th-century-canadian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=pulitzer-prize-winner">pulitzer-prize-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=scotland">scotland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3295434281" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15488698164703644170"><b>
  <blockquote>Life is an endless recruiting of witnesses. It seems we need to be observed in our postures of extravagance or shame, we need attention paid to us. Our own memory is altogether too cherishing, which is the kindest thing I can say for it. Other accounts are required; other perspectives, but even so our most important ceremonies - birth, love and death - are secured by whomever and whatever is available. What chance, what caprice!</blockquote>
</b><br/><br/><b>
  <blockquote>Birth, 1905; Childhood, 1916; Marriage, 1927; Love, 1936; Pho</blockquote></b></span>
  <span id="freeText15488698164703644170" style="display:none"><b>
  <blockquote>Life is an endless recruiting of witnesses. It seems we need to be observed in our postures of extravagance or shame, we need attention paid to us. Our own memory is altogether too cherishing, which is the kindest thing I can say for it. Other accounts are required; other perspectives, but even so our most important ceremonies - birth, love and death - are secured by whomever and whatever is available. What chance, what caprice!</blockquote>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>Birth, 1905; Childhood, 1916; Marriage, 1927; Love, 1936; Photo Album; Motherhood, 1947; Work, 1955-1964; Sorrow, 1965; Ease, 1977; Illness and Decline, 1985; Death.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />In this chronicle of a woman’s life, the complete ‘birth to death’ account of Daisy Goodwill Hoad Flett, author Carol Shields uses all of the biographer’s tools to build up her character. She delves into the lives of Daisy’s parents, she offers up the ‘evidence’ of facts, of lists and photos and details; she includes her sources - the letters and newspaper clippings, the speeches from important moments - and she takes into account the views of first-hand witnesses. Occasionally, she even ventures into the interior monologues of her subject. Occasionally, the first-person voice of Daisy peeks out and the object (of the novel’s attention) becomes subject. Even so, the reader is left with the impression that she doesn’t really know Daisy Goodwill very well at all. I would even suggest that Daisy does not know herself very well. <i>“She has a talent for self-obliteration.” “Daisy’s own thoughts on her marriage are not recorded.” “She just let her life happen to her.”</i><br /><br />The structure of this novel takes a straightforward chronology of a life and turns it into something so originally wonderful and strange. The stages of Daisy’s life, (or the lives of other characters, for that matter) seem entirely unconnected. “Things begin, things end.” Daisy is the epitome of an unremarkable 20th century life, and yet one is left with the impression of how eccentric people are - how unknowable - how mysterious. <br /><br />I first read this book in 1994 or 1995, when it was newly published. I was a young mother, with the feeling that the best part of my life was all in front of me. To reread it in 2020 was a kind of ‘deja vu’ of familiarity, without any memory of what would come next. I remembered mainly that I had loved it. I still love it, although Daisy herself remains elusive. <br /><br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>And the question arises: what is the story of a life? A chronicle of fact or a skilfully wrought impression? The bringing together of what she fears? Or the adding up of what has been off-handedly revealed, those tiny allotted increments of knowledge? She needs a quiet place in which to think about this immensity. And she needs someone - anyone - to listen.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>Never mind, it means nothing; it’s only Mrs. Flett going through the motions of being Mrs. Flett.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>Stone is how she finally sees herself, her living cells replaced by the insentience of mineral deposition.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />Read for the The Pulitzer Project. 4/12</span>
  <a data-text-id="15488698164703644170" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1090393865" />
    <a title="Jeanette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette"><img alt="Jeanette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372095557p2/11869072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1090393865?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanette" href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette">Jeanette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1090393865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10378883259025522689">Oh, how utterly substantial this ordinary life tale runs. Having read this in my middle years, I now reread it within a completely different life period and enjoyed it even more. How rich the language, without having to use foul chorus calls of trite ranting or other slurs. It's absolutely delightful to read words such as "larky" again.<br/> <br/>The life far less molded by electronic, media cultural standards. A life begun in great sorrow and also having patches of unsought fulfillment scattered along th</span>
  <span id="freeText10378883259025522689" style="display:none">Oh, how utterly substantial this ordinary life tale runs. Having read this in my middle years, I now reread it within a completely different life period and enjoyed it even more. How rich the language, without having to use foul chorus calls of trite ranting or other slurs. It's absolutely delightful to read words such as "larky" again.<br /> <br />The life far less molded by electronic, media cultural standards. A life begun in great sorrow and also having patches of unsought fulfillment scattered along the way. <br /><br />The aspects of the quarries and limestone use were also precious to me. I pass those sites at least 16 or 18 times a year and every single time that sight just mesmerizes me. Loaders and trucks on the shelves look like they are Tonka truck sized. Excellent, excellent feel of the Canadian and American Midwest during the first half of the twentieth century- from language to jobs to sensibilities to expressions of emotion. There were very, very few drama kings or queens. <br /><br />The photographs were awesome- they were never included in the first copy I read so many years ago. Real women's choices- you could feel them here. So many great under characters, both women and men. Just loved Clementine and Carol's rending of her feelings within her 20 year marriage.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10378883259025522689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

This time I read it as a writer, and although I found some faults with the story (it's the whole life of Daisy Flett) - too many extraordinary things happen, and yet not enough happens - I also really appreciated how well written it is, how Shields keeps us in suspense by using backstory, how she seamlessly flits arou</span>
  <span id="freeText923036255793995883" style="display:none">I read this when it first came out in paperback in 1994 (in fact it still has the Dillons sticker on the back), but I couldn't remember anything about it, except that I'd loved it. This time I read it as a writer, and although I found some faults with the story (it's the whole life of Daisy Flett) - too many extraordinary things happen, and yet not enough happens - I also really appreciated how well written it is, how Shields keeps us in suspense by using backstory, how she seamlessly flits around in time, and how she uses memory to great effect. </span>

<div id="review_3569874929" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3569874929" />
    <a title="Kiran Bhat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81560301-kiran-bhat"><img alt="Kiran Bhat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525335374p2/81560301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3569874929?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kiran Bhat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kiran Bhat" href="/user/show/81560301-kiran-bhat">Kiran Bhat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3569874929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer663910974144725783">A fairly prosaic and standard novel detailing the life of a woman named Daisy Flett. Admittedly, on a sentence level, it's quite good, but I don't feel that Shields lifts her concept above the simplicity implicit in her tale. It's good for people who like the comforting style of Ann Beattie or Anne Tylor, but not for those who like to see storytelling and language taken further than that.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4456042-alena?shelf=dusting-off-the-bookshelf">dusting-off-the-bookshelf</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer311827267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2559852867954058401">A true case of better late than never, I am so glad I finally read this book. It's both epic and humble, quiet and bold, a true masterpiece in both content and style.<br/><br/>We meet Daisy the moment she is born in 1905 and follow her life until it ends sometime in the 1990's. The book reads almost like an in-depth memoir, except that other perspectives (or versions of Daisy's story) keep breaking into the narrative. Shields also chooses the third person, even when we are reading Daisy's thoughts, which </span>
  <span id="freeText2559852867954058401" style="display:none">A true case of better late than never, I am so glad I finally read this book. It's both epic and humble, quiet and bold, a true masterpiece in both content and style.<br /><br />We meet Daisy the moment she is born in 1905 and follow her life until it ends sometime in the 1990's. The book reads almost like an in-depth memoir, except that other perspectives (or versions of Daisy's story) keep breaking into the narrative. Shields also chooses the third person, even when we are reading Daisy's thoughts, which keeps just the right amount of distance between the reader and the characters.<br /><br />Without being any sort of feminist track, the book is very much a woman's story of the 20th century. For the most part Daisy takes the "traditional" path, but that doesn't mean her life is without drama. "Orphaned" at birth, shuffled from Canada to the United States, widowed early and long-lived, Daisy faces each phase of her life searching to fill the void of her inner loneliness.<br /><br />"We accept, as a cosmic joke, the separate ways of men and women, their different levels of foolishness...Men, it seemed to me in those days, were uniquely honored by their stories that erupted in their lives, whereas women were more likely to be smothered by theirs."<br /><br />Shields has such a light writing hand, I felt more like I was peeking in on Daisy's life even though I was fully immersed in every thought and action. That's a tricky balance and Shields handles it beautifully.<br /><br />"In the middle of writing a check she forgets the month, then the year. She's gaga, a loon, she's sprung a leak, her brain matter is falling out like the gray fluff from mailing envelopes, it's getting all over her furniture." <br /><br />This is my second Shields novel, and again I am reminded how sad it is that her life was cut far too short by cancer.<br /><br />Read alikes<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1736739.Olive_Kitteridge" title="Olive Kitteridge by Elizabeth Strout" rel="nofollow noopener">Olive Kitteridge</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/74462.Unless" title="Unless by Carol Shields" rel="nofollow noopener">Unless</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2559852867954058401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50927651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1692649269823813638">"My mother's name was Mercy Stone Goodwill. She was only thirty years old when she took sick, a boiling hot day, standing there in her back kitchen, making a Malvern pudding for her husband's supper. A cookery book lay open on the table: 'Take some slices of stale bread,' the recipe said, 'and one pint of currants; half a pint of raspberries; four ounces of sugar; some sweet cream if available.' Of course she's didvided the recipe in half, there being just the two of them, and what with the scar</span>
  <span id="freeText1692649269823813638" style="display:none">"My mother's name was Mercy Stone Goodwill. She was only thirty years old when she took sick, a boiling hot day, standing there in her back kitchen, making a Malvern pudding for her husband's supper. A cookery book lay open on the table: 'Take some slices of stale bread,' the recipe said, 'and one pint of currants; half a pint of raspberries; four ounces of sugar; some sweet cream if available.' Of course she's didvided the recipe in half, there being just the two of them, and what with the scarcity of currants, and Cuyler (my father) being a dainty eater. A pick-and-nibble fellow, she calls him, able to take his food or leave it."<br /><br />A fat-free vanilla frozen yogurt version of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9328.The_House_of_the_Spirits" title="The House of the Spirits by Isabel Allende" rel="nofollow noopener">The House of the Spirits</a>'s dark chocolate Haagen Daz ice cream. That's pretty much all I can say about this book.<br /><br />Okay, I understand that this won the Pulitzer prize, and is therefore amazing. I recognize and appreciate that the writing is wonderfully done, and I had no trouble finishing this story and was glad I read it. But for some reason, it just never grabbed me in any meaningful way. It's a story about an ordinary woman, living an ordinary life, and dying an ordinary death. Nothing earth-shattering. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1692649269823813638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer984149249" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6023185304143930986">I've very ambivalent feelings about of this book. At places I think it's marvelously written and captivating as well as containing a number of nice and unusual narrative grips, such as telling an entire decade of the main characters life by only random correspondence (and only incoming letters, saved and read by the character herself). <br/><br/>At the same time, this and similar narrative techniques does not allow the reader to get to know the main character (and those around her) very well. A lot of act</span>
  <span id="freeText6023185304143930986" style="display:none">I've very ambivalent feelings about of this book. At places I think it's marvelously written and captivating as well as containing a number of nice and unusual narrative grips, such as telling an entire decade of the main characters life by only random correspondence (and only incoming letters, saved and read by the character herself). <br /><br />At the same time, this and similar narrative techniques does not allow the reader to get to know the main character (and those around her) very well. A lot of actions and choices stay unexplained. This, together with the fact that it is a short book (especially considering it tells of an entire life, close to 90 years) does make big parts of the book less interesting and at times it is hard caring very much what happens to and around the main character.</span>

<div id="review_192451154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/192451154" />
    <a title="Linda Hart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2455252-linda-hart"><img alt="Linda Hart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549256566p2/2455252.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/192451154?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda Hart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda Hart" href="/user/show/2455252-linda-hart">Linda Hart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer192451154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10072914493400445573">The ordinary life of an ordinary lady told in an extraordinary way. The characters develop and scenes unfold beautifully with nuances of language shifting from 1st to 3rd person and back again. In one instance the main character is describing her feelings and then you go on and read how her family and friends interpreted the same thing. The publisher calls it "seductive prose" which is fitting description, It won the 1995 Puitzer prize.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14316441466831733818">this is a terriffic novel. it is beautifully written and addresses some interesting ideas: the offhand catastrophes of everyday life; the way one person can casually devestate another without feeling a thing; the crime - and inevitability - of wasted time; the ability of women to suffer in silence to their dying breath; the impossibility of accurate autobiography; the sad ridiculousness of the idea that there is any justice to be had in this world. <br/><br/>the author treats her characters in this book a</span>
  <span id="freeText14316441466831733818" style="display:none">this is a terriffic novel. it is beautifully written and addresses some interesting ideas: the offhand catastrophes of everyday life; the way one person can casually devestate another without feeling a thing; the crime - and inevitability - of wasted time; the ability of women to suffer in silence to their dying breath; the impossibility of accurate autobiography; the sad ridiculousness of the idea that there is any justice to be had in this world. <br /><br />the author treats her characters in this book as stones whose characters are revealed as time and events chip away at them to reveal their inner being. <br /><br />the story is told without sentiment but with humor and wit. it is a book that is well worth reading. </span>

<div id="review_3514672362" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3514672362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer433997985629298434">On a summer day in 1905 in Manitoba, Mercy Goodwill goes into labor. She is both obese and naïve, and so she didn’t even know that she was pregnant. By the time her daughter, Daisy, emits her first cry, Mercy is dead.<br/><br/>The rest of this book is the story of Daisy and the people closest to her. She is raised for several years by a neighbor woman whose desertion of her husband is a main theme of the story. Then her father re-enters her life. Daisy’s first honeymoon ends tragically. But the rest of he</span>
  <span id="freeText433997985629298434" style="display:none">On a summer day in 1905 in Manitoba, Mercy Goodwill goes into labor. She is both obese and naïve, and so she didn’t even know that she was pregnant. By the time her daughter, Daisy, emits her first cry, Mercy is dead.<br /><br />The rest of this book is the story of Daisy and the people closest to her. She is raised for several years by a neighbor woman whose desertion of her husband is a main theme of the story. Then her father re-enters her life. Daisy’s first honeymoon ends tragically. But the rest of her life is quite ordinary. She marries again, raises a family, makes friends, has a late-life career. <br /><br />The story is told from Daisy’s perspective, but it’s an omniscient perspective. Daisy sees her mother on the day of her birth and has knowledge of her parents’ relationship that a daughter wouldn’t normally have. Unusual use of voice continues throughout the novel. At the beginning, Daisy tells her story in the first person. Then, for a while, the story is told in a mix of first and third person. Later scenes in the book are told exclusively in third person. But, whether first or third, the voice is always omniscient. Some parts of the story are told in the form of correspondence, or Daisy’s to-do or shopping lists. We have full access to all characters. <br /><br />What we don’t have access to is Daisy’s feelings, for the most part. We are never quite in Daisy’s head. Even when she is speaking in the first person, she is usually speaking of others, or of events happening outside of her. That made her an unsatisfying character for me. She’s always just out of reach. The reader never knows what she wants, and she lacks agency. She seems to drift through life, doing whatever others expect of her. <br /><br />This is a beautifully written book. The psychological insights are profound, and the language is gorgeous. But the main character ultimately felt insubstantial to me, so the book wasn’t as satisfying as it promised to be. Note that that’s a minority opinion – this book won a Pulitzer Prize.<br /><br />Like my reviews? Check out my blog at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.kathrynbashaar.com/blog/">http://www.kathrynbashaar.com/blog/</a><br />Author of The Saint's Mistress: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://camcatpub.com/thesaintsmistress">http://camcatpub.com/thesaintsmistress</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="433997985629298434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_53248846" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53248846" />
    <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska"><img alt="Elizabeth (Alaska)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304966095p2/2026178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53248846?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth (Alaska)" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska">Elizabeth (Alaska)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007666232" href="/book/show/11422118-the-stone-diaries">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=awards-pulitzer">awards-pulitzer</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53248846" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16133652857423218452">This was my first Carol Shields and it won't be my last, in spite of the fact that I would place this at the bottom of my 4-star reads (or even top of 3-star if I were feeling stingy). I thought the story/characters not especially enjoyable. The presentation was very interesting, however. Shields used a variety of ways to tell the story - different first person narratives, third person narratives, letters, and a mixture of newspaper articles. Some of what she wrote I read several times. <br/><blockquote>His voic</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText16133652857423218452" style="display:none">This was my first Carol Shields and it won't be my last, in spite of the fact that I would place this at the bottom of my 4-star reads (or even top of 3-star if I were feeling stingy). I thought the story/characters not especially enjoyable. The presentation was very interesting, however. Shields used a variety of ways to tell the story - different first person narratives, third person narratives, letters, and a mixture of newspaper articles. Some of what she wrote I read several times. <br /><blockquote>His voice is beautiful. Its texture is fine-woven wool. If it had a color it would be a warm chestnut. In tone, in fluidity, in resonance, it is all that a man's voice should be, with just that hint of Scottish burr, thinner than the skin of varnish on his oak lectern, giving necessary hardness. He rides straight up the walls of his sentences. His little pauses are sensuous gateways, without which his listeners would fall into a trance.</blockquote>If I were still young, I'd be searching for that fellow. And searching, and searching, and searching ...<br /><br />And then this paragraph caught me as I continue to spend several hours a month researching family history.<br /><blockquote>When we think of the past we tend to assume that people were simpler in their functions, and shaped by forces that were primary and irreducible. We take for granted that our forebears were imbued with a deeper purity of purpose than we possess nowadays, and a more singular set of mind, believing, for example, that early scientists pursued their ends with unbroken "dedication" and that artists worked in the flame of some perpetual "inspiration." But none of this is true.Those who went before us were every bit as wayward and unaccountable and unsteady in their longings as people are today.</blockquote>I'm not sure what the Pulitzer Committee was seeing that I don't, but I'm happy to have read this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16133652857423218452" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IdVI9YjlOCsxyuU0OtLkZFXQNtgnNMy0ZSepqVgf/6xcsieRKLSBt/mhqJq3hBVYxTj9qmBWnMGUqndWlWL8sQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Rdrqh2EZNjtWYC5LGU+PU/z061SM7+9QBkB7QUSxMtw4vYXjwUiPp54LY+WUGX5vbBwgJsuNvyX3zaW+icwxwQ==')})
  }
//]]>

<span id="freeTextContainer11330928898716198056">Carol Ann Shields was an American-born Canadian author. She is best known for her successful 1993 novel <i>The Stone Diaries</i>, which won the Pulitzer Prize for Fiction as well as the Governor General's Award. Her novel <i>Swann</i> won the Best Novel <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/award/show/1066-arthur-ellis-award' rel='nofollow noopener'>Arthur Ellis Award</a> in 1988.<br/><br/><br/></span>
